### [170] [How Hard is it to Rig a Benchmark? A Social Choice Analysis of Leaderboard Robustness](https://arxiv.org/abs/2605.23628)
*Polina Gordienko,Georg Schollmeyer,Frauke Kreuter,Christoph Jansen*

Main category: cs.LG

TL;DR: Benchmark-specific training can be viewed as election manipulation; the problem is NP-hard under Borda and mean win rate, and the paper derives instance-level robustness metrics for various aggregations, showing mean win rate is hardest to manipulate in MMLU/BBH experiments.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: As benchmarks increasingly drive ML progress, participants may strategically choose training data to inflate a model's leaderboard position. Formalizing this manipulation helps assess vulnerability and guide defenses.

Method: Map benchmark manipulation to shift bribery in ordinal elections. Prove NP-hardness of choosing datasets to make a target model top-ranked under Borda count and mean win rate. Define instance-level robustness (minimum number of datasets to ensure top ranking) and derive closed-form expressions for arithmetic mean, median, mean win rate, and pairwise majority. Empirically evaluate on MMLU (HELM) and BBH (Open LLM Leaderboard).

Result: NP-hardness established for Borda and mean win rate. Introduced instance-level robustness metrics; provided expressions for robustness under four aggregation methods. Empirical findings show mean win rate is most resistant to manipulation: on BBH with 24 tasks and 4507 models, median robustness is 22 tasks (92%) vs 13 tasks (54%) for arithmetic mean and 12 tasks (50%) for median/pairwise majority.

Conclusion: Benchmark manipulation is a computationally hard and quantifiable risk; mean win rate, despite being harder to manipulate, still exhibits measurable robustness gaps. The framework enables assessment and comparison of manipulation risk across benchmarks and suggests directions for more robust benchmark design and defense mechanisms.

Abstract: Multi-task benchmarks have become a central pillar of machine learning research, yet their growing influence has incentivised benchmark gaming -- strategic actions taken to improve the leaderboard rank of a specific model. Treating datasets as voters and models as candidates, we consider benchmark-specific training -- the inclusion of benchmark data in training -- as a form of election manipulation. For any ordinal benchmark, the problem of choosing datasets to train on so that a target model becomes top-ranked corresponds to shift bribery, a class of manipulation problems from computational social choice. Leveraging this identification, we show that the benchmark-specific training problem is NP-hard under Borda count and mean win rate. Complementing this worst-case perspective, we introduce the instance-level robustness, the minimum number of datasets a model developer must include in training to top a given leaderboard, and derive expressions for it under arithmetic mean, median, mean win rate and pairwise majority. We evaluate these expressions on MMLU under HELM and on BIG-Bench Hard (BBH) under the Open LLM Leaderboard. Across both suites, mean win rate is hardest to manipulate: this gap is clear on BBH (24 tasks, 4507 models), where its median robustness is 22 tasks (92%), compared with 13 (54%) under arithmetic mean and 12 (50%) under median and pairwise majority.

Main category: cs.LG

<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>

Method: Univariate marginals are modeled with normalizing flows (flexible density estimation). The joint dependence is captured via a Gaussian Mixture Copula, enabling a flexible correlation structure. The design enforces marginalization-consistency by construction.

Conclusion: CopFITi provides a principled, scalable framework for IMTS probabilistic forecasting by separating marginals and joint dependence with a marginalization-consistent copula, achieving strong joint density performance and better marginals compared to full joint models.

Abstract: We introduce CopFITi, a copula model for probabilistic forecasting of irregular multivariate time series (IMTS). Our model combines the expressivity of normalizing flows for univariate marginals with the consistency and flexibility of a Gaussian Mixture Copula for the joint dependency structure. Our experiments show that copula-based approaches, which decouple the marginals from the joint, yield better marginal models than architectures that directly fit the full joint. With CopFITi, we propose the first IMTS copula that is marginalization-consistent by construction and establish a new state of the art in joint IMTS density modeling.

Result: The Shannon Scaling Law provides higher fidelity fits (strong R^2) and captures loss basins that prior laws miss; it outperforms classical scaling laws and perturbation-aware laws under perturbations; demonstrates robust extrapolation from small models/tokens to larger, unseen scales (e.g., 12B→307B tokens) with pooled R^2 ≈0.847, whereas monotonic baselines collapse.

Abstract: Existing scaling laws for Large Language Models (LLMs), predominantly monotonic power laws, fail to explain emerging non-monotonic phenomena such as catastrophic overtraining and quantization-induced degradation, where performance deteriorates despite increased compute.
  We propose the Shannon Scaling Law, a unified theoretical framework that models LLM training as information transmission over a noisy channel, grounded in the Shannon-Hartley theorem. By mapping model parameters to channel bandwidth and training tokens to signal power, our formulation explicitly captures the interaction between learning signal and intrinsic noise. This perspective reveals a fundamental Shannon capacity for LLMs: scaling model size or data without preserving a sufficient signal-to-noise ratio (SNR) inevitably amplifies noise, inducing a transition from monotonic improvement to U-shaped performance degradation.
  We validate our theory through experiments on Pythia and OLMo2 under perturbations, including Gaussian noise, quantization and supervised fine-tuning on math, QA and code tasks. The Shannon Scaling Law consistently outperforms classical scaling laws and recent perturbation-aware laws, achieving strong $R^2$ scores and accurately capturing loss basins missed by prior approaches. It also extrapolates: fitted on $\leq$6.9B Pythia models with $\leq$180B tokens, it predicts the unseen 12B model up to 307B tokens at pooled $R^2{=}0.847$, while monotonic baselines collapse.

### [177] [Optimal Dimension-Free Sampling for Regularized Classification](https://arxiv.org/abs/2605.23726)
*Meysam Alishahi,Alexander Munteanu,Simon Omlor,Jeff M. Phillips*

Main category: cs.LG

TL;DR: Near-optimal sampling bounds for Lipschitz classification losses under regularization, with tight k^2/ε^2 and k/ε^2 scalings for L2 and L1 norms, respectively; for L2^2 regularization, linear-in-k bounds under certain derivative conditions; all upper bounds matched by lower bounds up to polylog terms; prior cubic k^3/ε^2 bounds are improved.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: To characterize dimension-free, optimal sampling complexity for efficient training of regularized classifiers with Lipschitz loss functions, across common regularizers and loss variants (logistic, hinge, sigmoid, ReLU).

Method: Develop uniform (or squared) norm sampling schemes and analyze their error via empirical process theory and higher-moment bounds. Derive upper and lower bounds depending on the regularizer (||·||_2/k, ||·||_1/k, ||·||_2^2/k) and properties of the generating function g (for the quadratic case), including derivative bounds, monotonicity/convexity, and g(0) conditions. Improve over prior sensitivity/VC-based approaches by avoiding overcounting.

Result: Provided upper and lower bounds for sampling complexity: k^2/ε^2 for ||·||_2/k, k/ε^2 for ||·||_1/k; for ||·||_2^2/k, linear-in-k sampling when g'(x) ≤ g(x), g(0)>0 and g is monotone or convex; otherwise k^2/ε^2; if g(0)=0, no dimension-free bounds exist. All bounds match up to polylog terms. Demonstrated improvement from k^3/ε^2 (cubic) to near-optimal rates via higher-moment empirical-process analysis.

Conclusion: The results reveal that sampling complexity in regularized Lipschitz classification is strongly governed by the regularizer and the derivative properties of the associated quadratic function g. The work tightens the landscape of dimension-free bounds, offering practical sampling schemes with provable guarantees and surpassing prior cubic-sample bounds by leveraging refined empirical-process techniques.

Abstract: We prove optimal sampling bounds achieving $(1\pm\varepsilon)$-relative error for a broad class of Lipschitz continuous classification loss functions under various regularization terms. This includes important functions such as logistic and sigmoid loss, hinge loss, and ReLU loss, as prominent and popular representative examples. In particular, we prove $k^2/\varepsilon^2$ upper and lower bounds for $\|\cdot\|_2/k$ regularization, and $k/\varepsilon^2$ upper and lower bounds for $\|\cdot\|_1/k$ regularization. For $\|\cdot\|_2^2/k$ regularization, the sampling complexity depends mainly on a bounded derivative property: if $|g'(x)|\leq g(x)$, and $g(0)>0$, and $g$ is monotonic or convex, then it admits linear in $k$ sampling complexity; otherwise the general bound is $k^2/\varepsilon^2$. However, if $g(0)=0$, our results indicate that no dimension-free bounds are possible, and even sublinear bounds are ruled out. All upper bounds are complemented by matching lower bounds up to polylogarithmic terms. Moreover, our work relies conceptually and algorithmically on simple uniform or (squared) norm sampling and hereby improves over recent cubic $k^3/\varepsilon^2$ sensitivity sampling bounds of (Alishahi and Phillips, ICML'24). This is achieved by refined arguments involving higher moment bounds and empirical process analyses to avoid overcounting that appears in the de-facto standard VC-dimension and sensitivity framework.

Result: Inspector improves validity to 100% (Opus) and 56% (Kimi); maintains near-baseline accuracy and strong generalization; models are physically valid, accurate, and extrapolate to unseen loads.

Conclusion: Separating generation from inspection yields trustworthy, scalable, physics-aware model discovery; technique-agnostic and adaptable to advances in LLMs.

Abstract: Developing constitutive models that capture how materials deform under load traditionally requires years of specialized expertise in continuum mechanics, machine learning, and scientific programming. Large language models (LLMs) have recently been shown to lower this barrier by generating constitutive models on demand, but existing single-agent pipelines lack systematic checks that the resulting models respect fundamental physical laws. To close this gap, we introduce the first multi-agent LLM-driven approach for constitutive model generation: a Creator agent proposes a model tailored to the data, while an Inspector agent critically audits each proposal against nine physical constraints and returns it for refinement whenever a violation is detected. We demonstrate this concept with constitutive artificial neural networks (CANNs) and benchmark it on brain tissue, experimental rubber, and synthetic rubber, using two different LLM backbones (Claude Opus 4.7 and Kimi K2.5). Adding the Inspector raises the share of exported models that truly satisfy all physical constraints from 91% to a perfect 100% for Opus and from 37% to 56% for Kimi, while preserving near-baseline accuracy and remarkable generalization to unseen loading paths. In combination, the generated models are physically valid, highly accurate, and extrapolate reliably beyond the training data - properties that together make them directly usable in practice. Separating generation from inspection thus turns LLM-driven constitutive modeling into a genuinely trustworthy process. The paradigm is deliberately technique-agnostic and scales automatically with advances in LLM capability, opening a promising path toward automated, physics-aware model discovery.

</details>


### [181] [Strong Teacher Not Needed? On Distillation in LLM Pretraining](https://arxiv.org/abs/2605.23857)
*Taiming Lu,Zhuang Liu*

Main category: cs.LG

TL;DR: Distillation in LLM pretraining can work with weak or mixed-strength teachers; strong teachers are not always beneficial and can saturate or reverse gains; distillation improves generalization more than in-domain performance, challenging the need for a strong teacher.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Test the prevailing assumption that a strong-to-weak teacher-student relationship is essential for effective knowledge distillation during LLM pretraining by exploring varying teacher strengths and token budgets.

Method: Vary architecture sizes and training token budgets to create strong-to-weak, same-level, and weak-to-strong teacher–student pairs. Train with a mixture of language modeling loss and knowledge distillation losses and evaluate on in-domain and generalization tasks.

Result: Small and undertrained teachers can improve larger students when using a proper mix of losses. Conversely, pushing the teacher to be stronger (more parameters or more tokens) can saturate or even reverse KD gains. Distillation yields greater improvements in generalization (OOD and downstream tasks) than in-domain fitting.

Conclusion: Distillation pretraining does not universally require a strong teacher. The optimal teacher strength is nuanced and context-dependent; combining losses allows small teachers to benefit large models, and the generalization gains from distillation can exceed those in-domain.

Abstract: Knowledge distillation generally assumes a strong-to-weak relationship where stronger teachers yield better students. In this work, we examine this assumption about distillation in large language model pretraining. By varying architecture sizes and training token budgets, we create strong-to-weak, same-level, and weak-to-strong teacher-student relationships, and study distillation's effectiveness under each. We find that the teacher need not be strong: with proper mixing of the language modeling and knowledge distillation losses, even small and undertrained teachers improve larger students. At the same time, a stronger teacher is not always better: pushing the teacher further, through more parameters or more training tokens, can saturate or even reverse the distillation gains. We further observe that distillation improves generalization (out-of-distribution and downstream performance) more readily than in-domain fitting. Together, these results challenge the common belief that distillation pretraining always requires a strong teacher.

</details>


### [182] [Training-Free Looped Transformers](https://arxiv.org/abs/2605.23872)
*Lizhang Chen,Jonathan Li,Chen Liang,Ni Lao,Qiang Liu*

Main category: cs.LG

TL;DR: Training-free test-time loop over a mid-stack block of a frozen transformer, using damped sub-steps, yields consistent accuracy gains without fine-tuning.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Address the reliance on training for looped transformers by introducing a test-time recurrence mechanism. Naive block reapplication can degrade performance; view pre-norm blocks as ODE steps and refine looping to improve accuracy across diverse models.

Method: Attach a lightweight inference-time wrapper that loops a contiguous mid-stack block of a frozen checkpoint, applying smaller damped sub-steps (OD-like refinement) instead of a single large update; no fine-tuning or architectural changes are required; demonstrated across seven model families (dense, sparse MoE, MLA+MoE).

Result: Across model families, achieving gains such as +2.64 pp on MMLU-Pro for Qwen3-4B-Instruct, +1.14 pp on CommonsenseQA for Qwen3-30B-A3B-Instruct, and +1.20 pp on OpenBookQA for Moonlight-16B-A3B-Instruct.

Conclusion: A training-free looped transformation of pretrained models via damped sub-steps yields consistent performance gains and validates test-time recurrence as a viable augmentation method, whereas naive block reapplication fails.

Abstract: We introduce training-free looped transformers, in which a lightweight inference-time wrapper loops a contiguous mid-stack block of layers of a frozen checkpoint without additional fine-tuning, continued training, or architectural changes. Unlike prior looped transformer methods that train with the looped structure end-to-end, we retrofit recurrence onto pretrained models at test time. We show that naive block reapplication usually degrades performance, highlighting the importance of the loop application strategy. Motivated by viewing a pre-norm transformer block as a forward Euler step on an ODE, we instead treat looping as a refinement of the same approximation, replacing one large update with smaller damped sub-steps. Across seven dense, sparse MoE, and MLA+MoE model families, our method improves Qwen3-4B-Instruct by +2.64 pp on MMLU-Pro, Qwen3-30B-A3B-Instruct by +1.14 pp on CommonsenseQA, and Moonlight-16B-A3B-Instruct by +1.20 pp on OpenBookQA.

</details>


### [183] [Complete-muE: Optimal Hyperparameter Transfer and Scaling for MoE Models](https://arxiv.org/abs/2605.23893)
*Hongwu Peng,Ohiremen Dibua,Yuanjun Xiong,Yifan Gong,Jianming Zhang,Yan Kang*

Main category: cs.LG

TL;DR: Two-bridge Complete-muE enables hyperparameter transfer across dense FFN, Dense MoE, and Sparse MoE in transformers, yielding a 'tune dense once, transfer to all' recipe with stable optima and faster convergence as capacity scales.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Hyperparameter tuning for MoE is hindered by architectural changes when scaling (Dense vs MoE, per-expert counts, routing). Existing tools μP require fixed architectures and SDE requires fixed per-step counts, so they cannot directly transfer across MoE configurations. A method that handles changes in activated experts, total capacity, granularity, and hybrids is needed.

Method: Bridge I: maps between dense FFN and Dense MoE using active-width μP with a normalized router scale. Bridge II: maps between Dense MoE and Sparse MoE via activated-expert scaling, where first-order SDE LR/WD corrections cancel, leaving a bounded residual σ0 shift. The combined transfer rule (Complete μE) accommodates changes in activated experts, capacity, granularity, and hybrids, as well as width/depth, batch size, and duration in general Transformers.

Result: Language model and diffusion model pretraining experiments show relatively stable hyperparameter optima across architectures and parameter counts, with small drift due to non-strict SDE behavior of Bridge II. Practically, tuning on a dense reference transfers effectively to all MoE configurations (tune dense once, transfer to all). MoE models exhibit accelerated convergence over dense models when scaling capacity, reducing hyperparameter search costs.

Conclusion: Complete-muE provides a robust, scalable framework for cross-architecture hyperparameter transfer in MoE-enabled transformers, enabling faster training and reduced search cost while supporting diverse MoE configurations.

Abstract: We propose Complete-muE, a framework which targets hyperparameter transfer across dense FFN and any Mixture-of-Experts (MoE) setups in transformer blocks. Existing tools such as $μ$P (requires fixed architectue) or SDE (requires fixed per-step token count) cannot directly solve the hyperparameter transfer problem in MoE setups because Dense to MoE transfer or MoE total experts scaling changes both architecture and tokens per expert. Complete-muE solves this challenge with a two-bridge system: Bridge~I maps between dense FFN and Dense MoE by active-width $μ$P with a normalized router scale. Bridge~II maps between Dense MoE and sparse MoE by activated-expert scaling, where the first-order SDE LR/WD correction cancels while a bounded residual $σ_0$ shift remains. The resulting transfer rule, which we term as Complete muE, covers changes in activated experts, total capacity, granularity, and shared/group-balanced hybrids for MoE models as well as network width/depth, batch size, and duration changes for general Transformer models. Extensive language model and diffusion model pretraining experiments confirm that complete-muE yields relatively stable hyperparameter optima across model architectures and parameter counts -- with only minor drift consistent with the non-strict SDE behavior of Bridge~II. In practice this drift is small enough that hyperparameters tuned on a single dense reference transfer near-optimally to all MoE configurations -- \emph{tune dense once, transfer to all} is the practical recipe at the core of Complete-muE. This enables MoE models to achieve accelerated convergence speedup over dense models when scaling model capacity without costly hyperparameter search.

</details>
